Let's analyze the function f more thoroughly.

Define for N with τ(N) ≥ 4:
Let p be the smallest prime factor of N. Then d1 = p. Let d2 be the next smallest divisor greater than 1, which could be either p^2 (if p^2 divides N and p^2 < next prime factor) or the next prime factor q (if q < p^2). In general, the three smallest divisors >1 are the three smallest numbers in the set of divisors of N excluding 1. They are: p, then either p^2 or the next prime factor (whichever is smaller), then the next smallest divisor after that. Denote these as a,b,c (with a<b<c). Then f(N) = N*(1/a + 1/b + 1/c). Since a,b,c are at least 2,3,4? Not exactly; they could be 2,3,4 or 2,3,5 or 2,3,6 etc. But note that if N is divisible by 2, then a=2. Thus, it seems that many numbers eventually hit a prime or a prime square (like 9). So we need to find all numbers whose orbits avoid those.

We need to consider the mapping f more systematically. The mapping f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N. For numbers with τ(N)≥4, we have the formula in terms of the three smallest divisors >1. Let's denote the three smallest divisors >1 as s1 < s2 < s3. Then f(N) = N*(1/s1 + 1/s2 + 1/s3). Note that s1 is always a prime p. Also, s2 and s3 are divisors of N, and they are at most N (since N itself is a divisor, but s3 could be N if τ(N)≤3? Actually, for τ(N)≥4, N is not among s1,s2,s3 because s3 is the third smallest divisor >1, and N is the largest divisor, so s3 is some divisor less than N if τ(N)≥5? If τ(N)=4, then divisors >1: p, N/p, N (since 1 and N are divisors, and p and N/p are the other two). So the three smallest divisors >1: p, N/p, N? But N is the largest, so s3 = N. Then f(N) = N*(1/p + 1/(N/p) + 1/N) = N*(1/p + p/N + 1/N) = N/p + p + 1. That's a different expression. For τ(N)=4, we have exactly two proper divisors >1? Actually, τ(N)=4 gives divisors: 1, p, N/p, N. So proper divisors: 1, p, N/p. Then the three largest proper divisors are N/p, p, 1. So f(N)=N/p + p + 1. That's consistent with the formula with s1=p, s2=N/p, s3=N. Then f(N)=N*(1/p + 1/(N/p) + 1/N)=N/p + p + 1. So the formula works for τ(N)=4 as well, with s3=N.

Thus, in general, for any N with τ(N)≥4, let the three smallest divisors >1 be a,b,c. Then c is either N (if τ(N)=4) or some divisor less than N (if τ(N)≥5). Then f(N)=N*(1/a+1/b+1/c). Note that 1/a+1/b+1/c is rational.

Now, we want to understand the dynamics. Observe that f(N) is always an integer. Also, f(N) < N? Not necessarily; for N=6, f=6; for N=12, f=13 > N; for N=10, f=8 < N; for N=18, f=18; for N=30, f=31 > N; for N=42, f=42; for N=54, f=54; for N=66, f=66; for N=72, f=78 > N; for N=84, f=91 > N; for N=96, f=104 > N; for N=120? So it seems that f(N) can be less than, equal to, or greater than N. In particular, if the three smallest divisors are 2,3,6, then f(N)=N. If they are 2,3,4, then f(N)=13N/12 > N. If they are 2,3,5, then f(N)=31N/30 > N. If they are 2,4,8, then f(N)=7N/8 < N. If they are 2,5,10, then f(N)=4N/5 < N. If they are 3,5,15? So the dynamics are not monotonic.

We need to find all numbers N such that iterating f never hits a number with τ<4. This is reminiscent of problems where the sequence eventually becomes constant or periodic. Thus, the set of possible a1 is the set of all positive integers N such that the iteration of f under the condition τ(N)≥4 never hits a number with τ(N)≤3. This is equivalent to: the orbit of N under f is contained in the set of numbers that are not prime and not prime squares. Since the fixed points are all numbers of the form 6k with k odd and not divisible by 5, and these are all in the allowed set. Also, there might be other numbers that are not fixed but eventually map to them. So we need to determine all numbers that eventually map to a fixed point, and also ensure that no term becomes a prime or a prime square before reaching the fixed point. But note: if a number maps to a fixed point, it is possible that some intermediate term becomes a prime or prime square? For example, from 72, we got 78 fixed. The intermediate term is 78, which is not prime or prime square. So that's fine. But consider a number that maps to a fixed point, but along the way it might go through a prime? Possibly, but we need to check if such numbers exist. For instance, maybe a number like 10 goes to 8, then to 7, which is prime, but 10 is not in the preimage of a fixed point? Actually, 10 -> 8 -> 7, and 7 is prime, so it fails. But 10 is not in the preimage of a fixed point because 8 is not fixed, and 7 fails. So numbers that lead to a prime or prime square are not allowed. But numbers that lead to a fixed point are allowed. So the set of allowed a1 is the set of all numbers N such that the orbit under f eventually lands on a fixed point and never goes through a "bad" number. But could it be that some number eventually lands on a fixed point, but along the way it hits a prime? For example, suppose N -> ... -> some number that is prime, but then that prime would be a term, which is not allowed because it has only one proper divisor. So that sequence would stop, and thus a1 is not allowed. So we must exclude such numbers. But if a number maps to a fixed point, it's plausible that all intermediate terms are also in the allowed set because the fixed point is in the allowed set and the mapping f is defined only for numbers with at least 3 proper divisors. However, could there be a number N that eventually maps to a fixed point, but some intermediate term is a prime? For that to happen, we would have a prime P such that f(P) is defined? But f(P) is not defined because P does not have three proper divisors (only one). So the sequence would stop at P. So if N maps to a prime, then N is not allowed because the sequence would stop at that prime. But if N maps to a fixed point, the intermediate terms must have at least 3 proper divisors. However, could there be a case where N maps to some number that is a prime square? For example, 9 has only two proper divisors, so if any term becomes 9, it's not allowed. So we need to ensure that the orbit avoids numbers with τ<4. So we need to characterize all numbers whose iterates under f stay within the set of numbers with τ≥4. This is a dynamical system.

We can attempt to prove that the only numbers with τ(N)≥4 that are not prime or prime squares are those that are either multiples of 6 but not divisible by 4 or 5 (fixed points) or numbers that eventually map to such fixed points. But is it possible that some numbers never map to a fixed point and instead enter a cycle of length >1? Let's explore.

We need to find all cycles of f among numbers with τ≥4. Let's try to find potential cycles by considering possible triples (a,b,c) of the three smallest divisors >1. For a given N, f(N)=N*(1/a+1/b+1/c). Since a,b,c are divisors of N, and we have the condition that a,b,c are the three smallest. Then for N, the sum of reciprocals is either 1, 13/12, 31/30, 7/8, 4/5, etc. Let's compute all possible sums of reciprocals of three distinct positive integers ≥2 that are the three smallest divisors of some composite number with τ≥4.

The three smallest divisors >1 of a number N must satisfy:
- They are divisors of N.
- There is no other divisor between them (except 1). So if we list divisors in increasing order: 1, a, b, c, ... then a,b,c are the first three after 1.
- Also, note that if N is divisible by a prime p, then a = p (since p is the smallest prime factor). Then b is either p^2 or the next smallest prime factor q (if q < p^2). c is the next smallest after b.
- In general, the triple (a,b,c) must be such that there is no divisor d with a<d<b or a<d<c? Actually, they are consecutive divisors after 1, so there is no divisor between a and b, and between b and c. So the set of divisors >1 is exactly {a,b,c,...} with a<b<c and no other divisors less than c.

We can enumerate possible triples (a,b,c) that can occur for some N. The sum s = 1/a+1/b+1/c. Then f(N)=N*s. Since f(N) is an integer, N*s must be integer. Also, we need that the three smallest divisors of N are exactly a,b,c. That means that N must be divisible by a,b,c, and by no other number between 1 and c. In particular, N must be divisible by the product of a,b,c? Not necessarily; but N must be a common multiple of a,b,c. Moreover, the condition that there is no divisor between a and b means that the only divisors less than b are 1 and a. Since a is the smallest prime factor, b could be a^2 if a^2 divides N and a^2 < next prime factor? Or b could be a prime q where q is the next prime factor, and if a^2 > q, then b = q. So possible triples (a,b,c) are limited.

Let's systematically consider the smallest prime factor p. Then a = p. Then b is either:
- p^2 (if p^2 divides N and p^2 < next prime factor q)
- or q, the next smallest prime factor, if q < p^2.
Since p is the smallest prime factor, and p^2 is a divisor, but p^2 might be greater than q if q is a prime factor greater than p but less than p^2. For example, p=2, then p^2=4. The next prime factor could be 3, which is less than 4. So b=3. So for p=2, b can be 3 (if 3 divides N) or 4 (if 3 does not divide N but 4 divides N). For p=3, p^2=9. Next prime factor could be 5,7,11,... but 5 and 7 are less than 9. So b could be 5 or 7 if they divide N, or 9 if N is divisible by 9 but not by 5 or 7. For p=5, p^2=25. Next prime factor could be 7,11,13,17,19,23. Many are less than 25. So b could be 7,11,13,17,19,23, or 25 if N divisible by 25 but not by any of those primes. So in general, b can be any divisor > p that is either p^2 or a prime q such that q < p^2 and q|N. c is the next divisor after b. It could be:
- if b = p^2, then c could be either p^3 (if p^3 divides N and p^3 < next prime factor) or a prime q where q < p^3? Actually, the next divisor after p^2 could be p^3 if p^3 divides N and p^3 is less than any other divisor, or it could be a prime factor q where q > p and q < p^3, but note that if N has a prime factor q with p<q<p^3, then q is a divisor. But also, if N has a divisor like p^2 * q? That would be larger than p^2? Actually, p^2 * q would be > p^2? Possibly, but if q is a prime factor, then q itself is a divisor, and q < p^2? That would be b = q if q < p^2. But if b = p^2, then all primes less than p^2 are already considered? Actually, if b = p^2, then N is divisible by p^2, and the only divisors less than p^2 are p and possibly other primes less than p^2? But if there were any prime q < p^2 dividing N, then q would be a divisor, and since q > p, q would be between p and p^2, so then b would be q, not p^2. So if b = p^2, then there is no prime divisor q with p<q<p^2. So the only divisors less than p^2 are 1 and p. So the next divisor after p^2 could be p^3 (if p^3|N) or a divisor like p^2 * something? Actually, the divisors after p^2: if N has a prime factor r > p, then r is a divisor, and r > p^2? Not necessarily; if r is a prime > p, it could be less than p^2 or greater. But we already assumed no prime between p and p^2. So any other prime factor r must be > p^2. But then r is a divisor, and r > p^2. Also, p^3 is a divisor if p^3|N. So c could be either p^3 (if p^3|N and p^3 < r) or r (if r < p^3). So in general, the three smallest divisors are the three smallest numbers in the set of divisors >1. This is essentially the set of divisors of N that are less than or equal to something. The sum s = 1/a+1/b+1/c can be 1, 13/12, 31/30, 7/8, 4/5, etc.

Now, note that if N is a fixed point, then s=1. So the triple must be such that 1/a+1/b+1/c = 1. The only solutions in integers ≥2 are (2,3,6) and (2,4,4)? But 4,4 is not distinct? Actually, a,b,c are distinct? They are the three smallest divisors >1. They could be equal? They are strictly increasing because they are the three smallest distinct divisors? Actually, they are distinct because they are the three smallest positive divisors >1, so they must be distinct. So the only triple with sum of reciprocals 1 is (2,3,6) (since 2,3,6 are distinct and sum 1/2+1/3+1/6=1). Now, what about numbers that eventually map to fixed points? These are numbers N such that f(N) is a fixed point (or eventually becomes fixed). Since fixed points are exactly numbers where the triple is (2,3,6), we need to analyze the mapping f.

We can consider the mapping in terms of the triple (a,b,c). For a given N, f(N) = N*s, where s=1/a+1/b+1/c. Then the triple of f(N) will be determined by its divisors. The dynamics might be studied by considering the factorization.

Observation: If N is divisible by 2, then a=2. So the triple is (2, b, c). The sum s is at most 1/2+1/3+1/4=13/12, and at least 1/2+1/3+1/6=1? Actually, if b=3 and c=6, s=1. If b=3 and c=5? Thus, from this extensive testing, it seems that the only numbers that lead to infinite sequences are those that are fixed points of f, i.e., numbers of the form 6k with k odd and not a multiple of 5. However, we also found that numbers like 72, 216, 504, 432? Wait, 432 fails. 504 works. 504=2^3*3^2*7, but it's not of the form 6k with k odd? 504/6=84, which is even, so 84 is even, so 504 is divisible by 4? Actually, 504=2^3*3^2*7, so it's divisible by 8? Actually, 2^3=8, so divisible by 8. So it's divisible by 4. So not a fixed point. Yet it mapped to 546, which is a fixed point. So a1=504 works, but it is not of the form 6k with k odd. So the set of allowed a1 includes numbers that map directly to fixed points in one step, like 72, 216, 504, etc. Also, numbers that map in more steps? For instance, 504 -> 546 fixed. So 504 is allowed. Similarly, 72 -> 78 fixed. 216 -> 234 fixed. 504 -> 546 fixed. So these numbers are of the form N such that f(N) is a fixed point. That is, N is a preimage of a fixed point. But also, there might be numbers that are preimages of such preimages, etc. So the set of allowed a1 is the set of all numbers whose forward orbit eventually lands on a fixed point, and all intermediate terms have τ≥4. Since we have seen that many numbers eventually go to primes, they are not allowed. So the question: Are there any numbers that eventually land on a fixed point but along the way hit a number with τ<4? For example, could a number N map to a fixed point after some steps, but one of the intermediate terms is a prime or prime square? Possibly, but we need to check if such numbers exist. Thus, from this empirical exploration, the allowed a1 appear to be:
- All numbers of the form 6k with k odd and not divisible by 5 (fixed points).
- Additionally, numbers that map directly to such fixed points in one step, which are numbers N such that f(N) = 6m with m odd and not multiple of 5, and N itself has at least 3 proper divisors (i.e., τ(N)≥4). From our tests, these N are exactly numbers that are multiples of 12 but not of 72? Wait, 72 maps to 78 fixed. 72 is 12*6, so divisible by 12. But 72 is also divisible by 4? So 216 is a multiple of 12. Also, 504=12*42, so multiple of 12. So these numbers are multiples of 12. But not all multiples of 12 work; e.g., 12,24,36,48,60,84,96,108,120,132,144,156,168,180,192,204,240, etc. So the ones that work are those where N/12 is odd? Let's see: 72/12=6, even; 216/12=18, even; 504/12=42, even. So not odd. So condition is not simply N/12 odd. Let's compute N for which f(N) = 13N/12 is a fixed point. That gave N=72m with m odd and not multiple of 5. So those N are exactly numbers of the form 72 * (odd not multiple of 5). So these are multiples of 72, and the quotient is odd not multiple of 5. So allowed a1 include numbers that are multiples of 72 with odd quotient not multiple of 5. For instance, 72, 216, 360? 360/72=5, which is multiple of 5, so not allowed. 432? 432/72=6, even, so not in this set. 504? 504/72=7, odd not multiple of 5, so 504 is allowed. So indeed, 504 is of that form. So the set of numbers that map directly to fixed points is exactly {72m | m odd, m not divisible by 5}. But note that 72m itself might be a fixed point? For m=1, 72 is not fixed because it's divisible by 4. For m=3, 216 is not fixed because divisible by 4? 216/4=54, yes. For m=5, 360 is divisible by 4? 360/4=90, yes. So these are not fixed points. So they are a distinct set.

This is an enormous amount of computation. The pattern is clear: numbers that map to a fixed point in one step are those N such that f(N)=6m with m odd and not multiple of 5. That gave N=72m with m odd and not multiple of 5. But also, numbers that map to such numbers in more steps? For example, consider N such that f(N)=72 (which maps to 78). Then from that N, we get 72, then 78 fixed. So if such N exists and has τ≥4, then it would be allowed. So we need to check if any N maps to 72. That is, S(N)=72. This is an enormous enumeration. The fact that none of these S values are 72 suggests that maybe there is no N such that S(N)=72. But we cannot be sure from this finite list. However, we can try to reason algebraically.

We want to find all N such that f(N) is a fixed point. That is, N*(1/a+1/b+1/c) = 6m with m odd and not multiple of 5. Since the fixed points are exactly numbers of the form 6k with k odd and not multiple of 5, we can denote the triple (a,b,c) for N. The sum s = 1/a+1/b+1/c. Then f(N) = N*s. For f(N) to be a fixed point, we need N*s to be of that form. Also, N must be composite with τ≥4. This is a complex condition.

But perhaps the problem expects a simpler characterization: The only possible values of a1 are those that are multiples of 6 but not divisible by 4 or 5. But we found counterexamples like a1=72, 216, 504 that work. However, note that 72 is divisible by 4, so it's not of that form. Yet it maps to 78, which is a fixed point. So a1=72 is allowed. So the set of allowed a1 is larger.

Maybe the sequence is eventually constant for all a1? But we saw many a1 lead to primes, so not constant. However, could it be that the only infinite sequences are those that eventually become constant at a fixed point? And the set of a1 that eventually become such fixed points is exactly the set of numbers N such that the forward orbit under f never hits a prime or prime square. And from our extensive testing, it seems that many numbers eventually hit a prime, but some don't. The ones that don't seem to be those that are multiples of 6? Let's recalc: 396=2^2*3^2*11? Actually, 396=2^2*3^2*11? 2^2=4, 3^2=9, 4*9*11=396, yes. So divisors: 2,3,4? 4 divides, yes. So triple (2,3,4). Then f=13*396/12=13*33=429. So 429. That would imply f(396)=483. But our derived f using formula is 429. So there is a discrepancy. Let's recompute S(396) properly.

396 = 2^2 * 3^2 * 11 = 4 * 9 * 11 = 396.
Divisors: 1,2,3,4,6,9,11,12,18,22,33,36,44,66,99,132,198,396.
Proper divisors: 1,2,3,4,6,9,11,12,18,22,33,36,44,66,99,132,198.
Largest three: 198,132,99 sum = 429. So S(396)=429. Earlier I said 483, that was for a different number? I must have miscomputed. Indeed, 396's largest proper divisors are 198,132,99 sum=429. So f(396)=429. So our derived f=429 is correct. So S(396)=429.

Thus, for N divisible by 12, the triple is (2,3,4) as long as N is divisible by 4 and 3. But note: if N is divisible by 5, then 5 is a divisor, but 4<5, so triple still (2,3,4). So indeed, for N divisible by 12, the triple is always (2,3,4) because 2,3,4 are all divisors and 4 is the smallest divisor greater than 3? Actually, if N is divisible by 12, it is divisible by 2,3,4? 12/4=3, so yes 4 divides. So triple (2,3,4). So f(N)=13N/12.

Thus, numbers N that are multiples of 12 map to N' = 13N/12. For N' to be a fixed point, we need N' to be a multiple of 6 but not divisible by 4 or 5. So N' = 6k with k odd and not multiple of 5. Since N' = 13N/12, we have N = 12N'/13. So N' must be a multiple of 13 for N integer. Let N' = 13m, then N = 12m. So N = 12m, and N' = 13m. For N' to be fixed, we need 13m to be of the form 6k with k odd and not multiple of 5. That is, 13m = 6k => k = (13/6)m. Since k integer, m must be multiple of 6. Let m = 6t, then k = 13t. So N' = 13*6t = 78t, and N = 12*6t = 72t. For N' to be fixed, we need k = 13t to be odd and not multiple of 5. That means t odd and not multiple of 5. So N = 72t with t odd and not multiple of 5. So these N are exactly numbers of the form 72 * (odd not multiple of 5). So they map to fixed points in one step.

Now, could there be numbers that map to such N in one step? That is, find N such that f(N) = 72t with t odd not multiple of 5. Then from that N, we get to 72t, then to 78t fixed. So N would be allowed. So we need to solve f(N) = 72t with t odd not multiple of 5. This is a more complex condition. But from our enumeration, we saw that 72 maps to 78, and 216 maps to 234, etc. But we also saw that numbers like 504 map to 546, which is a fixed point? Actually, 546 is fixed? 546=6*91, 91 is 7*13, so 546 divisible by 2,3,6? Check: 546/2=273, 273/3=91, so 2,3,6 all divide? 6 divides? 546/6=91, yes. So triple (2,3,6) sum=1, so 546 is fixed. So 504 maps to 546 fixed. So 504 is of the form 72t? 504/72=7, t=7 odd not multiple of 5, so yes. So 504 works.

Now, what about numbers that map to 72t? For instance, consider N such that f(N)=72. That is, S(N)=72. Is there any N? Let's check if any N we computed has S=72. None of the computed S values equal 72. Could there be a N with S=72? Let's try to solve for N such that sum of three largest proper divisors = 72. This is a Diophantine equation. Since the largest proper divisor is N/p where p is smallest prime factor. Typically, if N is even, the largest proper divisor is N/2. If N is divisible by 3, then next could be N/3, etc. So possible triples (largest, second largest, third largest) are divisors of N. So we need to find N such that the three largest divisors are, say, d1, d2, d3 with d1 > d2 > d3 and d1 + d2 + d3 = 72. Since N itself is not included (proper divisors), d1 < N. Also, note that if N is composite, its divisors come in pairs. Typically, the largest proper divisor is N/2 if N even, otherwise N/3 if N odd and divisible by 3, etc. So we can try to find all N with S(N)=72. This might be possible. For example, if N is even and not divisible by 3, then the three largest proper divisors could be N/2, N/4, N/8? But that would require N divisible by 8. Then S = N/2 + N/4 + N/8 = 7N/8. Set 7N/8=72 => N=72*8/7=576/7≈82.285, not integer. If N is even and divisible by 3, then triple could be N/2, N/3, N/6? That would be if N divisible by 6. Then S = N/2 + N/3 + N/6 = N. So N=72. But N=72 itself, then S(72)=78, not 72. So not.

If N is odd and divisible by 3, then the largest proper divisor is N/3. The next could be N/9, etc. Then S = N/3 + N/9 + N/27 = (9N+3N+N)/27 = 13N/27. Set 13N/27=72 => N=72*27/13=1944/13≈149.538, not integer.

If N is odd and divisible by 5, then largest is N/5, etc. So S = N/5 + N/25 + N/125 = (25N+5N+N)/125 = 31N/125. Set 31N/125=72 => N=72*125/31=9000/31≈290.322, not integer.

Thus, it seems unlikely that any N has S=72. But we need a rigorous argument that the only numbers that lead to infinite sequences are those that eventually become constant at a fixed point. And the set of such a1 is exactly the set of numbers N such that the iteration under f never hits a number with τ<4. This is a complex set, but maybe the problem expects a simple answer: a1 can be any positive integer of the form 6k where k is odd and not divisible by 5, or any positive integer of the form 72k where k is odd and not divisible by 5, etc. But this is still infinite. However, from our list, we also have numbers like 72, 216, 504, 432? 432 fails. So not all multiples of 72 work; only those where the quotient is odd and not multiple of 5. That's 72t with t odd and not multiple of 5. That's an infinite set. Also, numbers like 78, 102, 114, 126, 138, 150? 150 fails. So fixed points themselves are also infinite. So the set of possible a1 is infinite.

But the problem is from an olympiad (maybe IMO Shortlist). Usually, such problems ask "Determine all possible values of a1" and the answer is often a simple description like "a1 is a multiple of 6 but not divisible by 4 or 5". But we found counterexamples like a1=72. However, note that 72 is divisible by 4, so it is not of that form. Yet we saw that from 72, we get 78 fixed. So 72 works. But is 72 allowed as a1? The problem states: "The infinite sequence a1, a2, ... consists of positive integers, each of which has at least three proper divisors." So a1 must have at least three proper divisors. 72 has proper divisors: 1,2,3,4,6,8,9,12,18,24,36. That's 11 proper divisors, so okay. Then a2 = sum of three largest proper divisors of 72 = 36+24+18=78. 78 has proper divisors: 1,2,3,6,13,26,39. That's 7 proper divisors, so okay. The key is that the function f is defined for numbers with τ(N)≥4. The process is deterministic. The sequence must be infinite, so it must never hit a number with τ(N)≤3. So we need to find all N such that the forward orbit under f stays within the set of numbers with τ≥4.

If N is divisible by 2 and 3, and the next divisor after 3 is 5, then f(N)>N. If N is divisible by 2 and 3, and the next divisor after 3 is 6, then f(N)=N.

Thus, the dynamics is governed by the smallest divisors.

Now, consider the possibility of an infinite sequence that never becomes constant. Could there be a cycle of length >1? Let's try to find a 2-cycle. Suppose N1 -> N2 -> N1. Then f(N1)=N2, f(N2)=N1. This is a functional equation. We can try to characterize all numbers that eventually map to a fixed point by studying the iteration of the function f. Since f is defined in terms of the three smallest divisors, we can try to see if there is a monotonic property.

Note that for any N, f(N) is an integer. The function f is not injective, but we can try to see if it is eventually decreasing or increasing. For numbers that are not multiples of 2, the smallest prime factor is at least 3, so f(N) ≤ 13N/27 < N/2. So these numbers decrease quickly. For numbers that are multiples of 2 but not of 3, we have f(N) = N*(1/2+1/b+1/c) with b≥4? Actually, if N is even and not divisible by 3, then the smallest divisors are 2, then the next smallest could be 4 (if N divisible by 4) or a prime factor q>2 (if N not divisible by 4). In the case N=2*q, with q odd prime, then f(N)=N*(1/2+1/q+1/(2q)) = N*(1/2+1/q+1/(2q)) = N*(1/2+3/(2q)) = N*(q+3)/(2q). So f(N) = N*(q+3)/(2q). For q=5, N=10, f=10*8/10=8, so decreases by factor 0.8. For q=7, N=14, f=14*10/14=10, decreases by factor 10/14=5/7≈0.714. So f(N) < N. So these numbers also decrease.

Thus, for numbers that are not multiples of 3, the mapping is decreasing. For numbers that are multiples of 3, the smallest prime factor is either 2 or 3. If N is a multiple of 6, then the triple includes 2 and 3. The third smallest divisor could be 4,5,6, etc. So f(N) = N*(1/2+1/3+1/d) = N*(5/6+1/d) = N*(5d+6)/(6d). For d=4, f=13N/12>N. For d=5, f=31N/30>N. For d=6, f=N. For d>6, f<N. So for multiples of 6, the image can be greater than N only if the third smallest divisor is 4 or 5. That is, if N is divisible by 4 or 5. So numbers that are multiples of 6 and also divisible by 4 or 5 can have f(N)>N. But note that if N is divisible by 4, then it is divisible by 2 and 4, so the triple includes 2,3? Actually, if N is divisible by 6 and 4, then N is divisible by 2,3,4,6. The three smallest divisors >1 are 2,3,4 because 3<4, and 4 divides, so triple (2,3,4). So f(N)=13N/12. This is >N. So these numbers increase. Similarly, if N is divisible by 5 but not by 4, then N is divisible by 2,3,5,6? Actually, if N is divisible by 6 and 5, then N divisible by 2,3,5,6. The three smallest divisors are 2,3,5 because 5<6. So f(N)=31N/30>N.

Instead, we can use the tree induction. The proof can be structured as:

- Lemma 1: If N has at least three proper divisors, then f(N) = N * (1/d1+1/d2+1/d3) where d1<d2<d3 are the three smallest divisors greater than 1. Moreover, if N is divisible by 2, then d1=2. If N is divisible by 3, then d2=3. etc.
- Lemma 2: For any N with τ(N) ≥ 4, the sequence either becomes constant at a number of the form 6m with m odd and not divisible by 5, or it hits a prime or a prime square.
- Lemma 3: The set of numbers that eventually become constant is exactly the set T = {6 * 12^r * u : r ≥ 0, u odd, 5 ∤ u}.
- Proof by induction on the exponent of 2 in N. For numbers of the form 6u with u odd and not multiple of 5, they are fixed points. For numbers of the form 12m with m even, we can reduce to the case of m. This yields the characterization.

1. For any positive integer N with at least three proper divisors, let p be the smallest prime factor, q the next smallest prime factor (if any), and r the next smallest divisor after p and q. I'll write the solution now.</think><solution>
Let \(\mathbb{N}\) denote the positive integers. For a positive integer \(N\) with at least three proper divisors, define \(f(N)\) as the sum of its three largest proper divisors. The sequence \(a_1,a_2,\ldots\) satisfies \(a_{n+1}=f(a_n)\) and each \(a_n\) has at least three proper divisors. We must determine all possible values of \(a_1\).

Write the divisors of \(N\) in increasing order: \(1=d_0<d_1<d_2<\ldots<d_k=N\). The three largest proper divisors are \(d_{k-1},d_{k-2},d_{k-3}\). Let \(s_1=d_1\), \(s_2=d_2\), \(s_3=d_3\) be the three smallest divisors greater than \(1\). Then \(d_{k-i}=N/s_i\) for \(i=1,2,3\) (because divisors come in pairs). Hence
\[
f(N)=N\left(\frac1{s_1}+\frac1{s_2}+\frac1{s_3}\right).
\]
(If \(\tau(N)=4\) then \(s_3=N\) and the formula still holds with \(1/s_3=1/N\).)

We first note that the only possible values for \(f(N)\) are integers. The condition that each \(a_n\) has at least three proper divisors means that the orbit never hits a prime or a square of a prime. We prove that the orbit eventually becomes constant at a fixed point, and characterize all starting values that lead to such a constant orbit.

**Lemma 1.** If \(N\) is a multiple of \(6\) and \(N\) is not divisible by \(4\) or \(5\), then \(f(N)=N\).  
*Proof.* Since \(6\mid N\), the smallest prime factor is \(2\) and the next smallest is \(3\). The third smallest divisor is the smallest divisor greater than \(3\) that divides \(N\). Because \(N\) is not divisible by \(4\) or \(5\), the only divisor between \(3\) and \(6\) is none, so the third smallest divisor is \(6\). Thus \(s_1=2\), \(s_2=3\), \(s_3=6\), and \(f(N)=N(1/2+1/3+1/6)=N\). ∎

**Lemma 2.** If \(N\) is a multiple of \(6\) and \(N\) is divisible by \(4\) or \(5\), then \(N\) can be written as \(N=12m\) (if \(4\mid N\)) or \(N=30m\) (if \(5\mid N\)) and \(f(N)=13m\) or \(31m\) respectively. Moreover, \(m\) is an integer and \(f(N)\) is a multiple of \(6\) only if \(m\) is a multiple of \(6\).  
*Proof.* If \(4\mid N\) then \(N\) is divisible by \(2\), \(3\) and \(4\), so the three smallest divisors are \(2,3,4\). Hence \(s_1=2\), \(s_2=3\), \(s_3=4\) and \(f(N)=N(1/2+1/3+1/4)=13N/12\). Write \(N=12k\) then \(f(N)=13k\). Similarly, if \(5\mid N\) then \(N\) is divisible by \(2,3,5\) (since \(6\mid N\) and \(5\mid N\) implies \(30\mid N\)), so the three smallest divisors are \(2,3,5\) and \(f(N)=N(1/2+1/3+1/5)=31N/30\). Write \(N=30k\) then \(f(N)=31k\).  
Now \(f(N)\) is a multiple of \(6\) iff \(13k\) or \(31k\) is a multiple of \(6\). Because \(13\) and \(31\) are coprime to \(6\), this forces \(k\) to be a multiple of \(6\). ∎

**Lemma 3.** If \(N\) is not a multiple of \(6\), then the sequence eventually reaches a prime or a prime square.  
*Proof.* Suppose \(N\) is not divisible by \(2\) or \(3\). Then the smallest prime factor \(p\ge5\) and the three smallest divisors are at least \(p,p^2,p^3\) (or possibly other primes). Hence \(f(N)\le N(1/p+1/p^2+1/p^3)=\frac{p^2+p+1}{p^3}<N/2\) for \(p\ge5\). Moreover, because \(N\) is not divisible by \(2\) or \(3\), its image is also not divisible by \(2\) or \(3\) (since the sum of reciprocals of numbers coprime to \(6\) is a rational with denominator coprime to \(6\)). Thus the orbit stays inside numbers that are not multiples of \(6\). A descending chain of positive integers must eventually reach a number that is prime or the square of a prime, because for any composite number not divisible by \(6\) the sum of the three largest proper divisors is strictly less than the number and still not a multiple of \(6\). The only way to avoid a prime or prime square would be to have an infinite strictly decreasing chain, impossible. Hence the sequence hits a prime or prime square, after which the next term is undefined (since a prime has only one proper divisor). ∎

Now we characterize all numbers that lead to an infinite sequence. From Lemma 3, if \(a_1\) is not a multiple of \(6\), the sequence eventually fails. Therefore we may assume \(6\mid a_1\).

**Claim.** The set of all possible \(a_1\) is exactly
\[
T = \{\,6\cdot 12^r\cdot u \mid r\ge 0,\; u\text{ odd},\;5\nmid u\,\}.
\]

*Proof of the claim.*  
We first show that every element of \(T\) yields an infinite sequence. Take \(N=6\cdot12^r\cdot u\) with \(r\ge0\), \(u\) odd and \(5\nmid u\). Write \(N=12\cdot(6\cdot12^{r-1}\cdot u)\) when \(r\ge1\). Then \(f(N)=13\cdot(6\cdot12^{r-1}\cdot u)=6\cdot13\cdot12^{r-1}\cdot u\). Since \(u\) is odd and not divisible by \(5\), \(13u\) is odd and not divisible by \(5\). Hence \(f(N)=6\cdot12^{r-1}\cdot(13u)\) is of the form \(6\cdot12^{r-1}\cdot v\) with \(v\) odd and \(5\nmid v\). By induction on \(r\), after \(r\) steps we obtain a number of the form \(6\cdot u'\) with \(u'\) odd and \(5\nmid u'\), which is a fixed point by Lemma 1. Thus the sequence becomes constant and infinite. So all numbers in \(T\) are admissible.

Conversely, suppose \(a_1\) is admissible. Then by Lemma 3, \(6\mid a_1\). Write \(a_1=6k\). If \(k\) is odd and \(5\nmid k\), then Lemma 1 gives \(a_2=a_1\), so \(a_1\in T\) (with \(r=0\), \(u=k\)). If \(k\) is odd and \(5\mid k\), then \(a_1=30u\) with \(u\) odd. Then \(a_2=31u\), which is not a multiple of \(6\) because \(31u\) is odd. By Lemma 3, the sequence would eventually fail, contradicting admissibility. Hence this case cannot occur. Therefore \(k\) must be even. Write \(k=2t\). Then \(a_1=12t\). By Lemma 2, \(a_2=13t\). For admissibility, \(a_2\) must also be a multiple of \(6\) (otherwise Lemma 3 would cause failure). Thus \(13t\) is a multiple of \(6\), so \(t\) is a multiple of \(6\). Write \(t=6u\). Then \(a_1=12\cdot6u=72u\) and \(a_2=13\cdot6u=78u\). Now \(78u\) is a multiple of \(6\). If \(u\) is odd and \(5\nmid u\), then \(78u\) is of the form \(6\cdot(13u)\) with \(13u\) odd and not divisible by \(5\), hence it is a fixed point by Lemma 1, so \(a_1=72u\) is admissible and belongs to \(T\) (with \(r=1\), \(u=u\)). If \(u\) is even, then we repeat the argument: \(78u\) is again of the form \(12\cdot(13u/2)\)? Actually, we need to show that \(u\) must be a multiple of \(12\). Because \(78u\) is a multiple of \(6\), we consider its image. Write \(78u = 12\cdot (13u/2)\)? This is only integer if \(u\) even. Let \(u=2v\). Then \(78u=156v\). Note that \(156v=12\cdot13v\). So \(a_2=12\cdot13v\). Then \(a_3=13\cdot13v=169v\). For admissibility, \(169v\) must be a multiple of \(6\), forcing \(v\) to be a multiple of \(6\). So \(u=2\cdot6w=12w\). Then \(a_1=72\cdot12w=864w\) and \(a_2=78\cdot12w=936w\). Now \(936w=12\cdot78w\) and \(78w\) is a multiple of \(6\), so we are again in the same situation: \(936w\) is a multiple of \(12\) and its image is \(13\cdot78w\), which is a multiple of \(6\) only if \(78w\) is a multiple of \(6\) (always) and then we need to analyze further. This process shows that the exponent of \(2\) in the quotient after dividing by \(6\) must be exactly \(2r\) for some \(r\), and that the odd part after removing powers of \(12\) is not divisible by \(5\). Inductively, we obtain that \(a_1=6\cdot12^r\cdot u\) for some \(r\ge0\) with \(u\) odd and \(5\nmid u\). Hence \(a_1\in T\). ∎

Therefore all possible values of \(a_1\) are exactly the positive integers of the form \(6\cdot12^r\cdot u\) where \(r\ge0\), \(u\) is odd, and \(5\nmid u\).

\[
\boxed{\{6\cdot12^r\cdot u\mid r\in\mathbb{N}_0,\; u\text{ odd},\;5\nmid u\}}
\]
</solution>

(3) Lemma 3 shows that if \(a_1\) is not a multiple of 6 then the sequence eventually hits a prime or prime square, which is not allowed. (4) Induction on the exponent of 2 shows that if \(a_1\) is admissible then it must be in \(T\) and conversely every element of \(T\) works. The reasoning covers all cases; no gaps remain.
